Aaron collected their gold and fashioned a calf, declaring, "These are your gods, O Israel, who brought you up out of the land of Egypt!" (Exodus 32:4). The people worshiped the idol with sacrifices and revelry. God's anger burned. He told Moses He would destroy Israel and start over with Moses alone.

Moses interceded, and God relented from the worst of His judgment. But when Moses descended and saw the calf and the dancing, he shattered the tablets, ground the calf to powder, and made the people drink it. He called for those on the LORD's side to come to him. The sons of Levi responded, and Moses commanded them to kill the idolaters. Three thousand men died that day. Deuteronomy 9:20 later reveals that God's anger burned against Aaron specifically, and He would have destroyed him if Moses had not interceded.

Here is the pattern: Aaron committed idolatry while serving as God's appointed spokesman and priest. God's judgment nearly fell on him personally. Moses interceded. Aaron survived. And astonishingly, Aaron's priesthood continued. Not only did Aaron remain high priest, but the priesthood passed to his sons and remained in his line for generations. God's covenant purposes were not derailed by even this catastrophic failure. Hesed held firm through idolatry at the very foot of the mountain where God was giving the law.

This does not excuse Aaron's sin or suggest God treated it lightly. The three thousand deaths that day testified to the severity of God's holiness. But Aaron's survival and continued ministry testified to the resilience of God's covenant purposes. When God chooses someone for a role and binds Himself by covenant oath, even spectacular sin does not nullify that choice. Discipline comes—swift and severe. But the covenant holds because God holds it.

## Israel as a Nation (Exodus 19-40)

The corporate election of Israel receives its explicit formulation at Sinai. God brought the people to the mountain and declared through Moses: "You yourselves have seen what I did to the Egyptians, and how I bore you on eagles' wings and brought you to myself. Now therefore, if you will indeed obey my voice and keep my covenant, you shall be my treasured possession among all peoples, for all the earth is mine; and you shall be to me a kingdom of priests and a holy nation" (Exodus 19:4-6).

The language is tender and powerful. God bore them on eagles' wings—like a mother eagle carrying her young. He brought them to Himself—the goal was relationship, not merely rescue. They would be His treasured possession—the word segullah suggests a king's personal treasure, something valued above all else. The entire earth belonged to God, yet from all the nations, He chose Israel. The choice was particular, not universal. God loved Israel specifically.

Yet the covenant structure included both promise and stipulation. "If you will indeed obey my voice and keep my covenant" introduces the conditional element. But this conditionality operated within the framework of an already-established unconditional commitment. God had sworn to Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob that their descendants would be His people. That oath did not depend on Israel's obedience. But the experience of blessing within that covenant relationship did depend on obedience. Israel could not break the covenant in the sense of nullifying God's promises. But they could break covenant in the sense of violating its terms and thus experiencing curse rather than blessing.

The golden calf incident proved how quickly Israel would fail. Even while Moses was receiving the law, the people were breaking the first two commandments—worshiping an idol and calling it Yahweh. God's anger burned. He proposed starting over with Moses alone. Moses interceded, appealing to God's promises to Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob and to God's reputation among the nations. God relented from the full extent of judgment.

This pattern would repeat throughout Israel's history: rebellion, judgment, intercession, relenting, restoration. The covenant held not because Israel kept its terms, but because God remained faithful to His oath. Exodus 34 records the covenant renewal after the golden calf. God replaced the shattered tablets. He repeated His promises. He proclaimed His character—merciful, gracious, abounding in hesed. The relationship was restored not because Israel earned restoration but because God determined to continue what He had begun.

The construction of the tabernacle in Exodus 35-40 demonstrated God's commitment to dwell among His people despite their rebellion. He gave detailed instructions for the tabernacle's construction. The people brought offerings willingly. Skilled artisans crafted every element according to God's design. When the work was complete, the glory of the LORD filled the tabernacle. Exodus 40:34-35 records that the cloud covered the tent of meeting, and the glory of the LORD filled the tabernacle, so that Moses could not enter because the cloud settled on it, and the glory of the LORD filled the tabernacle.

God was dwelling among them. Not standing at a distance. Not relating only through judgment. He lived in their midst, in a tent, traveling with them through the wilderness. This was covenant love made visible. God chose to be present with a people who had already proven themselves rebellious. He instituted a sacrificial system that would provide atonement for their sins. He established a priesthood that would mediate between His holiness and their sinfulness. Every aspect of the tabernacle system testified that God was committed to maintaining relationship with people who would otherwise be consumed by His holy presence.

---

# LEVITICUS: Holiness and Covenant

Leviticus is often dismissed as irrelevant ritual law, but it reveals the deep structure of covenant relationship. God's holiness requires a means by which sinful people can remain in relationship with Him. The sacrificial system, the priesthood, the purity laws, and the great Day of Atonement all testify to one reality: God remains committed to dwelling among His people despite their ongoing sin. Leviticus is a book about hesed expressed through atonement.

## The Priesthood (Leviticus 8-10)

Aaron and his sons were consecrated as priests in an elaborate ceremony detailed in Leviticus 8. Moses washed them, clothed them in priestly garments, anointed them with oil, and offered sacrifices for their ordination. The process took seven days. On the eighth day, Aaron performed his first sacrifices as high priest. Fire came out from the LORD and consumed the burnt offering, and all the people shouted and fell on their faces (Leviticus 9:24). God's acceptance of the priesthood was visible and dramatic.

But the celebration was short-lived. Nadab and Abihu, Aaron's sons, "offered unauthorized fire before the LORD, which he had not commanded them. And fire came out from before the LORD and consumed them, and they died before the LORD" (Leviticus 10:1-2). The same fire that had just consumed the acceptable offering now consumed the priests who violated God's holiness. Moses told Aaron, "This is what the LORD has said: 'Among those who are near me I will be sanctified, and before all the people I will be glorified.'" Aaron remained silent.

God commanded Aaron and his remaining sons not to mourn or even leave the tabernacle that day. The priesthood could not stop functioning even in the face of personal tragedy. The work of atonement was too critical to be interrupted by grief. This reveals the seriousness of the priestly role and the danger of approaching God's holiness carelessly. Yet the priesthood continued through Aaron's remaining sons, Eleazar and Ithamar. The covenant institution survived even this catastrophic judgment.

The lesson is clear: God's covenant love does not compromise His holiness. Those who serve in His presence must do so on His terms. Nadab and Abihu's deaths were not arbitrary or cruel. They violated explicit commands regarding how to approach God's presence. The punishment fit the crime—they brought strange fire, and fire consumed them. Yet even this severe judgment operated within the framework of covenant. The priesthood was not abolished. Aaron remained high priest. The sacrificial system continued. God still dwelt among His people. Discipline was harsh, but the covenant held.

## The Nation's Relationship (Leviticus 26)

Leviticus 26 contains the covenant blessings and curses that would govern Israel's experience in the land. The structure is telling. If Israel obeyed, blessings would follow—rain in season, abundant crops, peace in the land, victory over enemies, and God's presence among them. "I will walk among you and will be your God, and you shall be my people" (Leviticus 26:12). The promise of God's presence remained the highest blessing.

If Israel disobeyed, curses would follow—disease, defeat, famine, wild beasts, pestilence, siege, and finally exile. The curses escalate in five waves of increasing severity, each introduced with "if you will not listen to me" or "if in spite of this you will not listen to me." The pattern suggests God's patient discipline, increasing the severity gradually to bring His people back to obedience.

But the most crucial verses come at the end. After describing exile and devastation so complete that the land itself would enjoy its Sabbath rest while lying desolate, God says: "Yet for all that, when they are in the land of their enemies, I will not spurn them, neither will I abhor them so as to destroy them utterly and break my covenant with them, for I am the LORD their God. But I will for their sake remember the covenant with their forefathers, whom I brought out of the land of Egypt in the sight of the nations, that I might be their God: I am the LORD" (Leviticus 26:44-45).

Even in exile, even after the worst curses have fallen, even when Israel has violated every covenant stipulation, God will not break His covenant. Why? "I will for their sake remember the covenant with their forefathers." The covenant with Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob stands beneath the covenant with Israel at Sinai. The unconditional oath grounds the conditional covenant. Israel can experience curse within the covenant, but they cannot break the covenant itself because the covenant ultimately depends on God's faithfulness to His own word, not Israel's faithfulness to their obligations.

This is hesed in its clearest form. God remains committed even when His people prove faithless. The commitment is rooted in His oath to the patriarchs and His own character. He acts "for His name's sake," not for Israel's sake. The covenant survives Israel's rebellion because it was never primarily about Israel's performance. It was always about God's glory displayed through His faithfulness to what He had sworn.

---

# NUMBERS: Wilderness Discipline

Numbers chronicles forty years of discipline in the wilderness. An entire generation died there because they refused to enter the Promised Land when God commanded them. Yet throughout those forty years, God sustained them daily with manna, water, and protection. The discipline was real and severe. The hesed was equally real and sustaining. Both coexisted because both flow from God's covenant character.

## The Wilderness Generation Rejected (Numbers 13-14)

At Kadesh-barnea, Israel stood on the border of the Promised Land. God commanded Moses to send spies to scout the land. Twelve men went—one from each tribe. They returned after forty days with a massive cluster of grapes requiring two men to carry it. The land was indeed flowing with milk and honey. But ten of the spies gave a terrifying report: the people were strong, the cities were fortified, and giants lived there. "We are not able to go up against the people, for they are stronger than we are" (Numbers 13:31).

Caleb and Joshua disagreed: "Let us go up at once and occupy it, for we are well able to overcome it" (Numbers 13:30). But the people believed the majority report. They wept all night. They grumbled against Moses and Aaron. They talked about appointing a new leader and returning to Egypt. They even threatened to stone Caleb and Joshua when they urged the people to trust God.

God's response was swift: "How long will this people despise me? And how long will they not believe in me, in spite of all the signs that I have done among them? I will strike them with the pestilence and disinherit them, and I will make of you a nation greater and mightier than they" (Numbers 14:11-12). For the second time, God proposed to start over with Moses alone. For the second time, Moses interceded, appealing to God's reputation among the nations and to His own self-proclaimed character as merciful and abounding in hesed.

God relented from destroying them entirely, but He did not relent from judgment. "Not one of the men who have seen my glory and my signs that I did in Egypt and in the wilderness, and yet have put me to the test these ten times and have not obeyed my voice shall see the land that I swore to give to their fathers. And none of those who despised me shall see it" (Numbers 14:22-23). Every person twenty years old and above at that time—except Caleb and Joshua—would die in the wilderness over the next forty years. Their children would enter, but they would not.

This judgment demonstrates God's particular choice in the starkest terms. An entire generation was passed over. God preserved the covenant line through their children, but the adults themselves were rejected from entering the rest God had promised. They would still be His people. He would still sustain them. He would still dwell among them in the tabernacle. But they would not receive the specific blessing of entering Canaan. That blessing passed to the next generation.

The pattern is clear: God's covenant with the nation held, but individuals within that covenant could be passed over for specific blessings based on their rebellion. The corporate election of Israel did not guarantee that every individual Israelite would experience every covenant blessing. God's particular love operated even within the chosen nation, selecting Caleb and Joshua for blessing while passing over the rest of their generation for judgment.

## Caleb and Joshua Chosen (Numbers 13-14, Joshua 14)

What distinguished Caleb and Joshua from the other spies? Numbers 14:24 gives the answer regarding Caleb: "But my servant Caleb, because he has a different spirit and has followed me fully, I will bring into the land into which he went, and his descendants shall possess it." Joshua receives similar commendation. But the question remains: where did this "different spirit" come from? Why did Caleb and Joshua trust God while the others did not?

The text attributes their faith to their own choice—they "followed me fully." Yet their ability to follow fully came from somewhere. Ten men saw the same land, the same giants, the same fortified cities. Ten men had the same history of witnessing God's miracles in Egypt and the wilderness. Yet ten said "we cannot" while two said "we are well able." The difference in response suggests a difference in what they were given to see. God granted Caleb and Joshua faith to trust Him. The other ten were given over to their unbelief.

This interpretation gains support from the fact that God had already determined the boundaries of the land and the peoples Israel would dispossess. He had already sworn to Abraham that his descendants would possess Canaan. The promise was unconditional. Israel's entry into the land was certain because God had sworn it. When ten spies said "we cannot," they were not merely expressing doubt about military strategy. They were contradicting God's explicit promise. Their unbelief was, at its core, disbelief in God's word.

Caleb and Joshua believed God's word would come to pass despite what seemed impossible from a human perspective. That belief was itself a gift. Hebrews 11:31-32 includes both Joshua and "Rahab the prostitute" in the hall of faith, indicating that their faith was counted as righteousness. But the question presses: where did the faith come from? The text in Numbers suggests God gave Caleb "a different spirit." The spirit within him was different from the spirit within the other ten. God made him different.

The reward for their faith was entrance into the land while their generation died in the wilderness. But even this reward required God's sustaining grace for forty years. Caleb was forty years old when he spied out the land. He was eighty-five when he finally received his inheritance (Joshua 14:10). Forty-five years passed between the promise and its fulfillment. God sustained him through all those years, keeping him strong enough to fight for his inheritance at an age when most men were feeble.

Joshua 14:10-12 records Caleb's testimony: "The LORD has kept me alive, just as he said, these forty-five years... And now, behold, I am this day eighty-five years old. I am still as strong today as I was in the day that Moses sent me; my strength now is as my strength was then, for war and for going and coming." God kept him alive. God kept him strong. God fulfilled what He had promised. Caleb's faith was met with God's faithfulness, and both the faith and the faithfulness originated in God's sovereign choice to give Caleb "a different spirit."

## Moses' Discipline (Numbers 20:1-13)

The incident at Meribah reveals how severely God disciplines those He loves most. The people quarreled with Moses over lack of water. They complained bitterly, wishing they had died in the wilderness or never left Egypt. God commanded Moses to take the staff, assemble the congregation, and "tell the rock before their eyes to yield its water" (Numbers 20:8).

But Moses was angry. He had endured forty years of complaints. He called the people rebels: "Hear now, you rebels: shall we bring water for you out of this rock?" (Numbers 20:10). Then instead of speaking to the rock as commanded, he struck it twice with his staff. Water came out abundantly. The people and livestock drank. The immediate crisis was resolved.

Yet God's response to Moses was devastating: "Because you did not believe in me, to uphold me as holy in the eyes of the people of Israel, therefore you shall not bring this assembly into the land that I have given them" (Numbers 20:12). For striking the rock instead of speaking to it, Moses forfeited the right to enter the Promised Land. Forty years of faithful leadership, countless intercessions for a rebellious people, face-to-face intimacy with God—none of it exempted Moses from this severe judgment.

The punishment seems disproportionate to the offense. Moses struck a rock in anger. Was that really worth being barred from Canaan after forty years of leadership? But the issue was not merely the physical action. God said Moses "did not believe in me, to uphold me as holy." In his anger, Moses had failed to represent God's character accurately. He spoke rashly ("shall we bring water for you?") as if he and Aaron were the source of the miracle rather than God. He acted in anger rather than reflecting God's patient grace. He struck rather than speaking, suggesting God's word alone was not sufficient to bring forth water.

God's holiness required vindication. Moses had failed to "uphold me as holy in the eyes of the people." The consequence was immediate and irrevocable. Moses would see the land from Mount Nebo, but he would not enter it. He would lead the people to the border, but Joshua would lead them across. The man who had known God face to face would die outside the land of promise.

This is hesed operating at full strength. God's covenant love includes severe discipline for His chosen servants who fail to represent Him accurately. The relationship between God and Moses remained—God continued speaking with him, working through him, and using him to lead Israel for decades after Meribah. Moses still interceded for the people. He still received God's commands. He still wrote the Torah. Face-to-face friendship continued. But the consequence of his failure also continued. Both realities coexisted because covenant love includes both intimate relationship and just discipline.

Deuteronomy 34:10 records the final assessment of Moses: "There has not arisen a prophet since in Israel like Moses, whom the LORD knew face to face." Personal intimacy with God did not prevent severe consequences for sin. Severe consequences for sin did not negate personal intimacy with God. Both were true simultaneously because hesed is not soft love. It is covenant faithfulness that both embraces warmly and disciplines harshly, depending on what the covenant requires.

## Balaam and Israel (Numbers 22-24)

The Balaam narrative demonstrates God's sovereign protection over His chosen people despite their ongoing rebellion. Balak, king of Moab, feared Israel and hired Balaam to curse them. Balaam was a pagan diviner with genuine contact with God—a strange figure who heard from God but also practiced divination. God told Balaam not to go with Balak's messengers and not to curse Israel "for they are blessed" (Numbers 22:12).

When Balak sent more distinguished princes with greater rewards, God permitted Balaam to go but commanded him to speak only what God told him to speak. On the way, God's anger burned against Balaam, and the angel of the LORD stood in the road to oppose him. Balaam's donkey saw the angel and turned aside three times. Balaam beat the donkey. Then God opened the donkey's mouth, and she spoke, rebuking Balaam for beating her. Finally, God opened Balaam's eyes to see the angel, who would have killed him if the donkey had not turned aside.

Balaam went to Moab. Balak brought him to overlook the Israelite camp and demanded he curse them. But when Balaam opened his mouth, blessings came out instead of curses. Four times Balak brought him to different locations to curse Israel. Four times God put words of blessing in Balaam's mouth instead. Numbers 23:19-20 captures the futility of trying to curse whom God has blessed: "God is not man, that he should lie, or a son of man, that he should change his mind. Has he said, and will he not do it? Or has he spoken, and will he not fulfill it? Behold, I received a command to bless, and he has blessed, and I cannot revoke it."

Numbers 23:21 gives God's reason for refusing to curse Israel: "He has not beheld misfortune in Jacob, nor has he seen trouble in Israel. The LORD their God is with them, and the shout of a king is among them." This is astonishing. The entire book of Numbers up to this point has chronicled nothing but misfortune and trouble in Israel. They have rebelled at every opportunity. They grumbled at the Red Sea. They complained about food and water. They built a golden calf. They refused to enter the land and talked about returning to Egypt. An entire generation was condemned to die in the wilderness.

Yet God says He has not beheld misfortune in Jacob. He sees no trouble in Israel. How can this be? Because God looks at Israel through the lens of His covenant promises. He sees them not according to their performance but according to His oath to Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. Their actual rebellion does not change His commitment to them. His hesed covers their sin. His promises stand despite their failures. He has determined to bless them, and no curse from a pagan diviner—no matter how powerful—can overturn what God has decreed.

This is sovereign protection. Israel did not earn it. They did not maintain it by their faithfulness. God maintained it by His faithfulness. Balaam tried four times to curse them and failed four times because God had chosen to bless them. The choice was sovereign, the protection was absolute, and the basis was God's covenant oath, not Israel's obedience. This is particular love demonstrated at the national level—God chose this people for blessing and would not allow them to be cursed, regardless of their actual merit or behavior.

---

# DEUTERONOMY: Covenant Renewed

Deuteronomy presents Moses' final addresses to Israel before his death. The new generation stands ready to enter the land their parents forfeited through unbelief. Moses rehearses the law, recounts their history, and emphasizes God's electing love as the sole explanation for their existence as God's treasured people.

## Israel's Election Explained (Deuteronomy 7:6-8)

The central text for understanding Israel's election appears in Deuteronomy 7:6-8: "For you are a people holy to the LORD your God. The LORD your God has chosen you to be a people for his treasured possession, out of all the peoples who are on the face of the earth. It was not because you were more in number than any other people that the LORD set his love on you and chose you, for you were the fewest of all peoples, but it is because the LORD loves you and is keeping the oath that he swore to your fathers, that the LORD has brought you out with a mighty hand and redeemed you from the house of slavery, from the hand of Pharaoh king of Egypt."

The passage explicitly rules out every possible human explanation for Israel's election. Not because they were numerous—they were the fewest. Not because they were powerful—they were enslaved. Not because they were righteous—the very next chapter will detail their stubbornness. The only explanation given is circular and tautological: "because the LORD loves you." Why does God love Israel? Because He loves them. Why did He choose them? Because He chose them.

The second reason given points back to God's oath to the patriarchs: He is "keeping the oath that he swore to your fathers." The choice of Israel flows from the choice of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. But this only pushes the question back one generation. Why did God choose those fathers? Genesis provides no explanation beyond God's sovereign will. The chain of causation ends in God's own free choice, grounded in nothing outside Himself.

This is the most explicit statement of unconditional election in the Old Testament regarding a nation. God chose Israel for no reason that can be found in Israel. The choice preceded their existence as a nation. It preceded any action they performed. It rested solely on God's sovereign decision to set His love on them. They were chosen to be His "treasured possession"—the same word (segullah) used in Exodus 19:5, suggesting a king's personal treasure that he values above all other possessions.

The implications are inescapable. If God chose Israel out of all the nations for no reason found in Israel, then God's love is particular and sovereign. If He set His love on them simply because He loves them, then His love is initiating and unconditional. If He redeemed them to keep the oath He swore to their fathers, then His love is covenantal and faithful. Every aspect of sovereign grace appears in these three verses.

## Not Because of Righteousness (Deuteronomy 9:4-6)

In case anyone might think Israel's election was based on their moral superiority, Deuteronomy 9:4-6 explicitly rules out that possibility. Moses warns them not to think, after God drives out the nations before them, "It is because of my righteousness that the LORD has brought me in to possess this land." He then gives the real reason: "It is because of the wickedness of these nations that the LORD is driving them out from before you, and that he may confirm the word that the LORD swore to your fathers, to Abraham, to Isaac, and to Jacob."

Moses then hammers the point: "Know, therefore, that the LORD your God is not giving you this good land to possess because of your righteousness, for you are a stubborn people." He proceeds to recount their rebellions: the golden calf at Horeb, the rebellion at Taberah, at Massah, at Kibroth-hattaavah, at Kadesh-barnea. "You have been rebellious against the LORD from the day that I knew you" (Deuteronomy 9:24).

The two reasons for Israel's inheritance are: (1) the wickedness of the nations God is dispossessing, and (2) God's oath to the patriarchs. Israel's own character does not enter the equation except negatively—Moses emphasizes that they are stubborn and rebellious, not righteous. They receive the land despite their character, not because of it. The gift comes from God's grace and God's oath, not from their merit or performance.

This pattern appears throughout Scripture. God's choice is never explained by reference to the qualities of the chosen. Abraham came from idolatry. Isaac was passive. Jacob was a deceiver. Moses was a murderer. David was an adulterer. Peter denied Christ. Paul persecuted the church. In every case, God's love preceded and produced whatever good appeared in the loved one. The good did not attract God's love. God's love created the good.

Deuteronomy 9 destroys any synergistic reading of Israel's election. Moses does not say, "God chose you because He foresaw you would choose Him." He does not say, "God chose you because He knew you would be righteous." He explicitly denies both possibilities. God chose them for His own reasons, to keep His own oath, to accomplish His own purposes. They contributed nothing but stubbornness and rebellion. God contributed everything else.

## Moses' Death (Deuteronomy 34)

The book ends with Moses' death on Mount Nebo. God brought him to the mountain, showed him the entire Promised Land, and told him, "I have let you see it with your eyes, but you shall not go over there" (Deuteronomy 34:4). Then Moses died there, and God buried him in the valley in the land of Moab, and "no one knows the place of his burial to this day" (34:6).

The final assessment of Moses emphasizes the intimacy of his relationship with God: "And there has not arisen a prophet since in Israel like Moses, whom the LORD knew face to face, none like him for all the signs and the wonders that the LORD sent him to do in the land of Egypt, to Pharaoh and to all his servants and to all his land, and for all the mighty power and all the great deeds of terror that Moses did in the sight of all Israel" (Deuteronomy 34:10-12).

Face to face. Personal knowledge. Intimate relationship. Yet he died outside the land because of one failure at Meribah. The harshness of the consequence did not nullify the warmth of the relationship. Both were true. God knew Moses face to face and excluded him from Canaan. God loved Moses as no other prophet and disciplined him severely. God used Moses to perform signs and wonders and refused to let him enter the land those signs and wonders secured.

This is hesed operating according to covenant terms. Love does not mean indulgence. Intimacy does not mean immunity from consequences. Face-to-face friendship does not exempt from discipline. God's covenant love includes all of this—personal warmth, severe discipline, intimate knowledge, harsh consequences, faithful promises, and uncompromising holiness. Moses experienced every aspect of hesed. He knew God's love more deeply than any prophet before him. He also experienced God's discipline more severely than most. Both flowed from the same covenant relationship.

God buried Moses personally. This final act of care from God Himself demonstrated that even in discipline, even in death outside the land, Moses remained beloved. God did not delegate the burial to Joshua or the elders. He took Moses' body and buried it Himself in a secret location. The intimacy continued even past death. The man who knew God face to face was cared for by God's own hands even in burial.

This is the God of Scripture. He loves His chosen ones with fierce, holy, unwavering commitment. He disciplines them when they fail to uphold His holiness. He holds them accountable for their actions. Yet He never abandons them, never revokes His choice, never stops loving them. Moses died outside Canaan but not outside God's love. The consequence was real. The relationship was equally real. Both were sustained by hesed—covenant love that is both tender and terrible, both warm and unbending, both personal and holy.

---

# JOSHUA: Conquest and Faithfulness

Joshua succeeds Moses by God's appointment, not by natural succession or democratic election. The book demonstrates God's faithfulness to His covenant promises as Israel finally enters the land sworn to Abraham four centuries earlier. Individual relationships continue the pattern established in Genesis and Exodus—God chooses, sustains, and accomplishes His purposes through people who contribute nothing but obedience enabled by His grace.

## Joshua (Joshua 1-24)

God's commission to Joshua began with a promise grounded in His prior relationship with Moses: "Just as I was with Moses, so I will be with you. I will not leave you or forsake you" (Joshua 1:5). Joshua received no independent calling based on his own qualifications. His leadership flowed from God's covenant commitment to continue what He had begun with Moses. The promise of God's presence was unconditional—"I will be with you, I will not leave you." Joshua's success depended entirely on God's faithfulness to that promise.

The conquest of Jericho demonstrated this dependence. God gave Joshua specific, seemingly absurd instructions: march around the city once a day for six days, then seven times on the seventh day, blow trumpets, and shout. The walls would collapse. No military strategy. No siege engines. No human effort could take credit for the victory. The people obeyed, the walls fell, and Israel took the city. The victory belonged to God alone. Joshua's role was to obey the instructions. God's role was to accomplish the impossible.

Yet Joshua's leadership was not without failure. The Gibeonites deceived him with moldy bread and worn-out clothes, pretending to be from a distant country. Joshua 9:14 records the critical error: "So the men took some of their provisions, but did not ask counsel from the LORD." Joshua made a treaty with them, swearing an oath before discovering they lived nearby and should have been destroyed. The oath bound Israel. They could not break it even though it had been obtained through deception.

God's response is telling. He did not nullify the treaty or excuse Israel from keeping it. The oath stood. Years later, when Saul violated this treaty by attacking the Gibeonites, God sent famine on Israel until David made atonement (2 Samuel 21). God honored the oath Joshua swore even though it resulted from failure to seek counsel. This reveals something crucial about God's covenant commitments: He keeps His word even when His people fail to seek His guidance before making promises.

The sun standing still at Gibeon (Joshua 10:12-14) demonstrated God's power operating in response to Joshua's prayer. When Joshua needed more daylight to complete the battle, he commanded the sun and moon to stand still. "And the sun stood still, and the moon stopped, until the nation took vengeance on their enemies... The sun stopped in the midst of heaven and did not hurry to set for about a whole day. There has been no day like it before or since, when the LORD heeded the voice of a man, for the LORD fought for Israel." God controlled the cosmos to give Joshua victory. The relationship was personal enough that God "heeded the voice of a man."

Joshua's farewell addresses in chapters 23-24 emphasized that every victory, every successful conquest, every fulfilled promise came from God's action, not Israel's strength. "You know in your hearts and souls, all of you, that not one word has failed of all the good things that the LORD your God promised concerning you. All have come to pass for you; not one of them has failed" (Joshua 23:14). God's covenant faithfulness had been proven through every battle. Joshua then recounted Israel's history from Abraham forward, showing that God had initiated, sustained, and completed everything.

The covenant renewal at Shechem in Joshua 24 concluded with Joshua's warning: "You are not able to serve the LORD, for he is a holy God" (24:19). This seems like strange leadership—telling the people they cannot do what he is commanding them to do. But Joshua understood the reality: serving God requires God's enabling grace. Left to themselves, the people would turn to other gods. Only God's covenant commitment to them would keep them faithful. Joshua's warning proved prophetic. The book of Judges would chronicle their repeated failures immediately after his death.

## Rahab (Joshua 2, 6:22-25)

Rahab's inclusion in Israel demonstrates God's sovereign grace extending beyond ethnic boundaries. She was a Canaanite prostitute living in Jericho—about as far outside the covenant community as possible. Yet when the Israelite spies came to Jericho, she hid them, risking her own life. Her confession in Joshua 2:9-11 revealed faith: "I know that the LORD has given you the land... for the LORD your God, he is God in the heavens above and on the earth beneath."

Where did this faith come from? Rahab had heard reports of what God did at the Red Sea and to the Amorite kings. But so had everyone else in Jericho. The king heard the same reports and responded by trying to capture the spies. Rahab heard the reports and responded by hiding them and confessing faith in their God. The difference in response suggests a difference in what they were given to see. God granted Rahab faith to recognize His power and align herself with His people.

Hebrews 11:31 confirms this interpretation: "By faith Rahab the prostitute did not perish with those who were disobedient, because she had given a friendly welcome to the spies." Her faith saved her. But faith comes from somewhere. Romans 10:17 says faith comes from hearing, and hearing through the word of Christ. Rahab heard about God's mighty acts and believed. The hearing produced faith, but God gave the hearing and granted the faith that followed.

The spies made a covenant with Rahab: "Our life for yours! If you do not tell this business of ours, then when the LORD gives us the land we will deal kindly and faithfully with you" (Joshua 2:14). The word for "kindly and faithfully" combines chesed and emeth—covenant love and truth. The spies bound themselves to show her the same covenant faithfulness God showed Israel. When Jericho fell, they kept their oath. Rahab and her entire family were brought out safely and lived among Israel.

Matthew 1:5 includes Rahab in the genealogy of Christ: "Salmon the father of Boaz by Rahab." A Canaanite prostitute became an ancestor of King David and ultimately of Jesus. Her inclusion testifies to God's sovereign freedom to extend saving grace to whomever He chooses, regardless of ethnic background or moral history. She contributed nothing but the faith God gave her to exercise. God contributed everything else—the hearing, the faith, the protection, the inclusion, and the placement in the Messianic line.

## Caleb's Inheritance (Joshua 14:6-15)

Forty-five years after the Kadesh-barnea incident, Caleb claimed his inheritance. He was eighty-five years old. Joshua 14:8 records his testimony: "I wholly followed the LORD my God." This was the same assessment God gave in Numbers 14:24—Caleb had "a different spirit" and "followed me fully." Now, after forty-five years of waiting, Caleb stood ready to take possession of what God had promised.

The remarkable element is Caleb's sustained strength. "And now, behold, I am this day eighty-five years old. I am still as strong today as I was in the day that Moses sent me; my strength now is as my strength was then, for war and for going and coming" (Joshua 14:10-11). God had kept him alive and kept him strong for forty-five years specifically so he could inherit what had been promised. This was not natural vigor. This was supernatural preservation for covenant purposes.

Caleb asked for Hebron—not an easy inheritance, but one inhabited by the Anakim, the very giants that had terrified the ten spies forty-five years earlier. At eighty-five, Caleb was ready to fight giants for his inheritance. Joshua blessed him and gave him Hebron. Joshua 14:14 notes that "Hebron therefore became the inheritance of Caleb... because he wholly followed the LORD, the God of Israel." The inheritance came because of whole-hearted following, but the whole-hearted following came from the "different spirit" God gave him.

God's faithfulness to Caleb across decades demonstrates hesed operating at the individual level within corporate election. All Israel was chosen, but within that chosen nation, God sustained specific individuals for specific purposes. Caleb's faith at Kadesh-barnea was rewarded with entrance to the land. His sustained faithfulness through forty-five years of wilderness wandering was rewarded with supernatural strength to conquer his inheritance. Both the initial faith and the sustained faithfulness came from God's enabling grace. Both were met with God's covenant faithfulness that kept every promise made.

## Achan Judged (Joshua 7)

The conquest of Ai initially failed because Achan had taken devoted things from Jericho—things God had commanded be destroyed or given to the treasury. God's anger burned against Israel. Thirty-six men died in the failed assault on Ai. Joshua tore his clothes and fell on his face, asking God why He had brought them across the Jordan only to give them into Amorite hands.

God's answer revealed the problem: "Israel has sinned; they have transgressed my covenant that I commanded them; they have taken some of the devoted things; they have stolen and lied and put them among their own belongings" (Joshua 7:11). Note the corporate language—"Israel has sinned." One man sinned, but the whole nation bore the guilt until the sin was dealt with. This demonstrates the corporate solidarity of the covenant community. Individual sin affected the entire people.

When Achan was identified by lot, he confessed: he saw a beautiful cloak from Shinar, two hundred shekels of silver, and a bar of gold, and he coveted them and took them. Joshua pronounced judgment: "Why did you bring trouble on us? The LORD brings trouble on you today" (Joshua 7:25). Achan, his sons and daughters, his oxen, donkeys, sheep, tent, and all that he had were brought to the Valley of Achor. All Israel stoned him and burned them with fire.

The severity of the judgment shocks modern sensibilities. But the issue was covenant violation in a context where God's holiness was being displayed to the nations. Israel was conquering Canaan as God's instrument of judgment on wickedness. If they themselves violated God's commands regarding the devoted things, they became indistinguishable from the nations they were dispossessing. God's holiness required vindication. The covenant stipulations required enforcement. Achan's judgment served as a warning to all Israel that God's commands were not suggestions.

Yet the nation itself was preserved. After Achan's sin was dealt with, Israel successfully conquered Ai. The covenant held. God continued fighting for Israel. The discipline was severe, but it operated within the covenant relationship. God did not abandon Israel because of one man's sin. He required the sin be addressed, and once addressed, the relationship continued. This is hesed expressed through justice—covenant love that disciplines severely to maintain the holiness necessary for God's presence among His people.

---

# JUDGES: Cycles of Rebellion and Deliverance

Judges chronicles Israel's repeated pattern of rebellion, oppression, crying out, and deliverance. The cycle repeats throughout the book: Israel does evil, God gives them into the hand of enemies, they cry out, God raises up a judge to deliver them, they have rest, then after the judge dies they return to evil. The pattern demonstrates both Israel's persistent faithlessness and God's persistent hesed. He delivers them again and again despite their repeated returns to idolatry.

## Gideon (Judges 6-8)

When God called Gideon, Israel was hiding in caves from Midianite oppression. The angel of the LORD appeared to Gideon and said, "The LORD is with you, O mighty man of valor" (Judges 6:12). Gideon's response revealed his doubt: "Please, my lord, if the LORD is with us, why then has all this happened to us? And where are all his wonderful deeds that our fathers recounted to us?" (6:13). God's presence seemed absent. His past deliverances seemed irrelevant. Gideon questioned whether God was truly with them.

God's answer did not address Gideon's questions. Instead, He gave a commission: "Go in this might of yours and save Israel from the hand of Midian; do not I send you?" (6:14). Gideon protested: "Please, Lord, how can I save Israel? Behold, my clan is the weakest in Manasseh, and I am the least in my father's house" (6:15). The protest mirrored Moses' objections at the burning bush. Gideon saw his own inadequacy clearly. But God's call did not depend on Gideon's adequacy. God's presence would make the difference: "But I will be with you, and you shall strike the Midianites as one man" (6:16).

Gideon requested a sign. God graciously provided it—fire consuming the offering Gideon prepared. Then God commanded Gideon to tear down his father's altar to Baal and cut down the Asherah beside it. Gideon obeyed, but did so at night because he feared his family and the townsmen. When they discovered what he had done the next morning, they wanted to kill him. His father defended him, saying if Baal was a god, let Baal contend for himself. The town's response revealed the depth of Israel's idolatry—Gideon's own family worshiped Baal.

The fleece tests in Judges 6:36-40 demonstrated Gideon's persistent doubt alongside God's patient accommodation. Gideon asked for a sign: let the fleece be wet with dew while the ground around it remained dry. God granted it. Then Gideon asked for the reverse—let the fleece be dry while the ground was wet. God granted that too. These requests revealed Gideon's weakness of faith, but God accommodated his weakness rather than rejecting him for it. The relationship was personal enough that God patiently worked with Gideon's doubts.

When Gideon assembled an army, God reduced it from thirty-two thousand to three hundred. God told him explicitly why: "The people with you are too many for me to give the Midianites into their hand, lest Israel boast over me, saying, 'My own hand has saved me'" (Judges 7:2). God would not allow Israel to attribute the victory to their own strength. The final army of three hundred carried torches in jars and blew trumpets. When they broke the jars and shouted, the Midianite camp erupted in confusion, and they killed each other. The victory was obviously God's. No one could claim human strength won the battle.

Yet Gideon's later failure revealed the persistence of sin even in those God uses mightily. After the victory, Gideon made an ephod from the gold taken as plunder. Judges 8:27 records: "Gideon made an ephod of it and put it in his city, in Ophrah. And all Israel whored after it there, and it became a snare to Gideon and to his family." The man who tore down Baal's altar created an object that became an idol. God used him to deliver Israel, but Gideon's heart remained divided. The deliverance came from God's power, not Gideon's righteousness. God accomplished His purposes through a deeply flawed man.

## Samson (Judges 13-16)

Samson's story demonstrates God's sovereign purposes accomplished through a man characterized by lust, violence, and covenant-breaking. An angel announced Samson's birth to his barren mother, declaring, "Behold, you shall conceive and bear a son. No razor shall come upon his head, for the child shall be a Nazirite to God from the womb, and he shall begin to save Israel from the hand of the Philistines" (Judges 13:5). Before conception, God designated Samson for a specific task. The "shall begin to save" indicates Samson would not complete the deliverance, only initiate it.

The Nazirite vow required abstaining from wine, avoiding corpses, and never cutting one's hair. Samson violated every aspect of it. He touched a dead lion. He drank wine at his wedding feast. He gave his hair to Delilah, breaking the final restriction. His entire life was characterized by breaking the vows God placed on him from the womb. Yet the Spirit of the LORD came upon him repeatedly, empowering him for supernatural feats of strength.

Samson's relationships with women dominate the narrative. He saw a Philistine woman in Timnah and demanded his parents get her for him as a wife. "Get her for me, for she is right in my eyes" (Judges 14:3). His parents objected—she was a Philistine, outside the covenant community. But Judges 14:4 reveals God's hidden purpose: "His father and mother did not know that it was from the LORD, for he was seeking an opportunity against the Philistines." God used even Samson's lust to accomplish His purposes against Israel's enemies.

After this woman betrayed him, Samson burned Philistine grain fields in revenge. The Philistines responded by burning the woman and her father. Samson killed many Philistines. They came to capture him, and the men of Judah bound him and handed him over to avoid Philistine oppression. As the Philistines approached, "the Spirit of the LORD rushed upon him, and the bonds on his arms became as flax that has caught fire" (Judges 15:14). He broke free, found the jawbone of a donkey, and killed a thousand men with it.

The Delilah incident revealed Samson's persistent foolishness. She asked three times for the source of his strength, and each time Samson lied. Each time she tested his answer and called the Philistines. Each time he escaped. Yet when she pressed him the fourth time, complaining he did not love her, Samson revealed the truth: "A razor has never come upon my head, for I have been a Nazirite to God from my mother's womb. If my head is shaved, then my strength will leave me, and I shall become weak and be like any other man" (Judges 16:17).

Delilah had his hair cut while he slept. When he awoke and tried to break free as before, "he did not know that the LORD had left him" (Judges 16:20). The Philistines seized him, gouged out his eyes, bound him with bronze shackles, and made him grind at the mill in prison. The mighty judge of Israel was now blind, enslaved, and mocked. But the text notes: "the hair of his head began to grow again after it had been shaved" (16:22).

The Philistines brought Samson out to mock him at a festival to their god Dagon. Samson stood between the pillars supporting the temple. Judges 16:28 records his final prayer: "O Lord GOD, please remember me and please strengthen me only this once, O God, that I may be avenged on the Philistines for my two eyes." God heard. Samson pushed the pillars, the temple collapsed, and he killed more Philistines in his death than during his life.

What are we to make of Samson? He violated every aspect of his Nazirite vow. He consorted with prostitutes. He was betrayed repeatedly yet kept trusting the same type of woman. He used his God-given strength for personal revenge rather than Israel's liberation. Yet God's Spirit empowered him throughout. God answered his final prayer from the ruins of his life. God accomplished His purposes through this moral catastrophe of a man. The deliverance of Israel did not depend on Samson's righteousness. It depended on God's sovereign determination to use him despite his persistent sin.

Hebrews 11:32 includes Samson in the hall of faith. This is astonishing. Samson's life was characterized by faithlessness, yet he is counted among those who "through faith conquered kingdoms, enforced justice, obtained promises." The faith counted to him must have been the faith God gave him to pray that final prayer. God gave him enough faith in that moment to cry out for strength one last time. God answered by bringing down the temple. The entire story testifies that God's purposes are accomplished by God's power, not human virtue. Samson's inclusion in Hebrews 11 demonstrates that God's hesed extends even to those whose lives are marked by catastrophic moral failure.

## Other Judges

The pattern holds across the other judges. Othniel was Caleb's younger brother, chosen and empowered by the Spirit to deliver Israel from Cushan-rishathaim (Judges 3:7-11). Ehud killed Eglon king of Moab by deception, stabbing him in his belly and escaping (3:12-30). Deborah, a prophetess, summoned Barak to lead Israel's army against Sisera, but Barak refused to go without her, resulting in the glory going to a woman—Jael, who killed Sisera with a tent peg (4:1-5:31). Each judge was chosen by God, empowered by His Spirit, and used to accomplish deliverance despite personal limitations.

Jephthah's story combines divine calling with human foolishness resulting in tragedy (Judges 11). He was the son of a prostitute, driven away by his half-brothers, living as an outlaw. When the Ammonites attacked, the elders of Gilead called Jephthah back to lead them. Before battle, Jephthah made a rash vow: "If you will give the Ammonites into my hand, then whatever comes out from the doors of my house to meet me when I return in peace from the Ammonites shall be the LORD's, and I will offer it up for a burnt offering" (11:30-31).

God gave him victory. When Jephthah returned home, his only daughter came out dancing to meet him. He tore his clothes in anguish, but held himself bound by the vow. The text's assessment is ambiguous—it does not explicitly condemn or commend the vow, but the tone suggests tragedy. Jephthah's daughter accepted her fate, asking only for two months to weep over her virginity. Then Jephthah did according to his vow.

What are we to learn from this? God did not stop Jephthah from making a foolish vow. God gave him victory despite the vow. The tragedy that followed resulted from human rashness, not divine command. Yet God's purposes for Israel were accomplished—the Ammonites were defeated, Israel had rest. God worked through Jephthah's leadership despite Jephthah's foolish vow. The pattern holds: God's sovereign purposes advance through deeply flawed human instruments. He does not wait for perfect people to accomplish His will. He works through the flawed, empowers the weak, and accomplishes His purposes despite human failure.

---

# RUTH: Gentile Included in Covenant

The book of Ruth provides a counterpoint to Judges' darkness. Where Judges chronicles Israel's repeated apostasy, Ruth tells of covenant faithfulness demonstrated by a Moabite woman. Her inclusion in Israel and placement in David's genealogy demonstrates God's sovereign grace extending to the most unlikely recipients.

## Ruth (Ruth 1-4)

Naomi's family fled to Moab during famine. Her husband died. Her two sons married Moabite women—Orpah and Ruth. Then both sons died, leaving Naomi with two foreign daughters-in-law and no male heirs. When Naomi heard the famine in Judah had ended, she determined to return. She urged her daughters-in-law to return to their mothers' houses and remarry among their own people. Orpah reluctantly left. Ruth clung to Naomi.

Ruth's words in 1:16-17 became the definitive expression of covenant commitment: "Do not urge me to leave you or to return from following you. For where you go I will go, and where you lodge I will lodge. Your people shall be my people, and your God my God. Where you die I will die, and there will I be buried. May the LORD do so to me and more also if anything but death parts me from you." This was not merely family loyalty. This was conversion. Ruth identified herself with Israel's people and Israel's God. She took an oath in the LORD's name, binding herself to Naomi until death.

Where did this faith come from? Ruth was a Moabite, descended from Lot's incestuous union with his daughter. Moab was excluded from the assembly of the LORD to the tenth generation (Deuteronomy 23:3). Yet Ruth confessed faith in Yahweh and committed herself to His people. The text gives no explanation for her conversion. She simply believed. Naomi's witness during their years together must have planted seeds, but why did Ruth believe while Orpah did not? Both women heard the same witness, lived in the same household, married into the same family. Only Ruth clung to Naomi and confessed faith in Naomi's God.

The most likely explanation is God's sovereign grace. God gave Ruth faith to believe. He drew her to Himself through Naomi's witness. He put it in her heart to cling to Naomi when Orpah turned back. Everything that followed flowed from that initial gift of faith. Ruth's subsequent actions demonstrated the reality of her conversion, but the conversion itself was God's work.

When Ruth and Naomi returned to Bethlehem, Ruth went to glean in the fields. "And she happened to come to the part of the field belonging to Boaz, who was of the clan of Elimelech" (Ruth 2:3). The phrase "happened to come" suggests chance from a human perspective, but the narrative makes clear this was divine providence. Boaz noticed Ruth, asked about her, and showed her extraordinary kindness. He commanded his young men not to touch her, told her to drink from their water, and invited her to eat with his workers. He even instructed his workers to pull out grain from the bundles and leave it for her to glean.

Ruth asked why she had found favor in his eyes, seeing she was a foreigner. Boaz's answer revealed he knew her story: "All that you have done for your mother-in-law since the death of your husband has been fully told to me, and how you left your father and mother and your native land and came to a people that you did not know before. The LORD repay you for what you have done, and a full reward be given you by the LORD, the God of Israel, under whose wings you have come to take refuge!" (Ruth 2:11-12). Boaz recognized Ruth's conversion and her refuge under the LORD's wings. He prayed that God would reward her faithfulness.

But the narrative shows God rewarding her through Boaz himself. Naomi realized Boaz was a kinsman-redeemer—a relative with the right and responsibility to marry Ruth and redeem Elimelech's property. Naomi instructed Ruth to approach Boaz at night on the threshing floor and lie at his feet. Ruth obeyed. When Boaz awoke and found her there, she said, "Spread your wings over your servant, for you are a redeemer" (Ruth 3:9). The same word Boaz used about God's wings, Ruth now used about Boaz's garment. She was asking him to be the means by which God would provide her refuge.

Boaz agreed but noted there was a closer relative with first right of redemption. The next day, Boaz met with this relative at the city gate. When the relative learned he would have to marry Ruth and that any son would carry on the name of the dead rather than his own name, he declined. Boaz then redeemed the property and took Ruth as his wife before witnesses. The transaction was legal, proper, and public. Everything was done according to covenant law.

Ruth 4:13 records the result: "So Boaz took Ruth, and she became his wife. And he went in to her, and the LORD gave her conception, and she bore a son." The LORD gave conception. The child was God's gift. They named him Obed. Ruth 4:17 reveals his significance: "He was the father of Jesse, the father of David." A Moabite woman became the great-grandmother of Israel's greatest king. Matthew 1:5 places her in the genealogy of Christ: "Salmon the father of Boaz by Rahab, and Boaz the father of Obed by Ruth, and Obed the father of Jesse, and Jesse the father of David the king."

Two Gentile women—Rahab the Canaanite prostitute and Ruth the Moabite widow—appear in the Messianic line. Both were outside the covenant community by birth. Both came to faith in Israel's God. Both were included in Israel through God's sovereign grace. Both became ancestors of David and ultimately of Jesus. Their inclusion testifies that God's electing love extends beyond ethnic Israel to whomever He chooses. The choice was His. The faith was His gift. The inclusion was His purpose. They contributed nothing but the obedience that resulted from the faith He gave them.

## Naomi (Ruth 1-4)

Naomi's story demonstrates God's hesed through bitter loss. She left Bethlehem full—husband and two sons. She returned empty—widowed, childless, bereft. Ruth 1:20-21 records her bitterness: "Do not call me Naomi ['pleasant']; call me Mara ['bitter'], for the Almighty has dealt very bitterly with me. I went away full, and the LORD has brought me back empty. Why call me Naomi, when the LORD has testified against me and the Almighty has brought calamity upon me?"

Naomi interpreted her suffering as God's judgment. She saw His hand in her losses. This interpretation may or may not have been accurate, but her theology was sound—she recognized God's sovereignty over all circumstances, including suffering. She did not attribute her losses to chance or fate. She saw the LORD's hand in everything that happened. Her response was lament, not denial.

Yet the narrative shows God was working toward restoration even through the losses. Ruth's loyalty stemmed from Naomi's witness during the years in Moab. If Naomi had not maintained her faith through widowhood and loss, Ruth might never have converted. If the family had never gone to Moab, Ruth would never have married Naomi's son. If Naomi's sons had not died, Ruth would not have returned to Bethlehem as a childless widow available for Boaz to redeem. Every loss, while genuinely painful, was woven into God's larger purposes.

The women of Bethlehem celebrated when Obed was born: "Blessed be the LORD, who has not left you this day without a redeemer, and may his name be renowned in Israel! He shall be to you a restorer of life and a nourisher of your old age, for your daughter-in-law who loves you, who is more to you than seven sons, has given birth to him" (Ruth 4:14-15). Naomi, who went out full and came back empty, was now filled again. The grandson born to Ruth was placed in Naomi's arms, and the women said, "A son has been born to Naomi" (4:17).

God's hesed persisted through Naomi's bitterness and loss. He did not answer her lament immediately or explain His purposes. He simply worked through circumstances to bring about restoration in His timing. Naomi's faith, tested through suffering, remained intact enough to instruct Ruth in how to approach Boaz. Her bitterness did not destroy her relationship with God or her wisdom in covenant matters. God's covenant love sustained her through years of loss and brought her to restoration she could not have imagined when she returned to Bethlehem empty and bitter.

---

# 1 SAMUEL: Kings Appointed and Rejected

First Samuel chronicles the transition from judges to monarchy. Samuel serves as the last judge and first prophet of the monarchical period. Saul is chosen as first king, then rejected. David is chosen while still a youth to replace him. The book demonstrates that God's choice can include temporary appointments to office that do not reflect eternal election, while also showing the warmth and permanence of God's love for those He chooses for salvation. Not all who are chosen for service are chosen for sonship.

## Samuel (1 Samuel 1-3)

Samuel's birth came in answer to Hannah's desperate prayer. She was barren, mocked by her rival wife, and deeply grieved. At Shiloh, she prayed with such intensity that Eli the priest thought she was drunk. Her vow was specific: "O LORD of hosts, if you will indeed look on the affliction of your servant and remember me and not forget your servant, but will give to your servant a son, then I will give him to the LORD all the days of his life, and no razor shall touch his head" (1 Samuel 1:11). She asked for a son and promised to give him back to God's service.

First Samuel 1:19-20 records God's response: "And Elkanah knew Hannah his wife, and the LORD remembered her. And in due time Hannah conceived and bore a son, and she called his name Samuel, for she said, 'I have asked for him from the LORD.'" The text explicitly attributes Samuel's conception to God's remembrance and action. Hannah asked, God answered. When Samuel was weaned, Hannah brought him to Shiloh and presented him to Eli. Her song of thanksgiving in 1 Samuel 2:1-10 celebrated God's sovereignty over life and death, poverty and wealth, exaltation and humiliation. She recognized that everything came from God's hand.

Samuel grew up in the tabernacle under Eli's tutelage. First Samuel 2:26 notes, "Now the boy Samuel continued to grow both in stature and in favor with the LORD and also with man." The language parallels what would later be said of Jesus in Luke 2:52. God's favor rested on Samuel from childhood. When God called Samuel one night, the boy did not recognize the voice at first. After the third call, Eli realized God was speaking to the boy and instructed him to respond, "Speak, LORD, for your servant hears" (1 Samuel 3:9).

God's message to Samuel concerned judgment on Eli's house for the wickedness of his sons and Eli's failure to restrain them. The message was difficult for a child to deliver to the priest who raised him, but Samuel told Eli everything. From that night forward, "the LORD was with him and let none of his words fall to the ground. And all Israel from Dan to Beersheba knew that Samuel was established as a prophet of the LORD" (1 Samuel 3:19-20). God's call came in childhood, and God's presence remained with Samuel throughout his life.

The relationship between God and Samuel was characterized by intimacy and faithfulness. First Samuel 3:21 records, "And the LORD appeared again at Shiloh, for the LORD revealed himself to Samuel at Shiloh by the word of the LORD." God continued appearing to Samuel, revealing Himself, speaking His word. The prophetic ministry Samuel exercised for decades flowed from God's initiative in calling him as a child. Samuel did not seek the role. God appointed him to it before he could even recognize God's voice. The choice was sovereign, the calling was clear, and the relationship was sustained by God's continued revelation.

## Saul Rejected (1 Samuel 9-15)

Saul's story demonstrates that God can choose someone for office and position without choosing them for salvation. God told Samuel, "Tomorrow about this time I will send to you a man from the land of Benjamin, and you shall anoint him to be prince over my people Israel" (1 Samuel 9:16). Saul came looking for lost donkeys. God arranged the meeting. Samuel anointed him. The Spirit of God rushed upon him, and he prophesied among the prophets. Everything about Saul's selection appeared to indicate God's favor.

Saul's initial humility reinforced this impression. When Samuel identified him as the one the LORD had chosen, Saul responded, "Am I not a Benjaminite, from the least of the tribes of Israel? And is not my clan the humblest of all the clans of the tribe of Benjamin? Why then have you spoken to me in this way?" (1 Samuel 9:21). When Samuel prepared to present him publicly as king, Saul hid among the baggage. When Samuel found him and presented him, the people shouted, "Long live the king!" But some worthless fellows despised him and brought him no present. Saul held his peace.

The turning point came with partial obedience. In 1 Samuel 13, facing the Philistine army with his men deserting, Saul waited seven days for Samuel to come and offer sacrifice as Samuel had instructed. When Samuel delayed, Saul took it upon himself to offer the burnt offering. As soon as he finished, Samuel arrived. Samuel's rebuke was severe: "You have done foolishly. You have not kept the command of the LORD your God, with which he commanded you. For then the LORD would have established your kingdom over Israel forever. But now your kingdom shall not continue" (1 Samuel 13:13-14).

The final breach came in 1 Samuel 15. God commanded Saul to destroy the Amalekites completely—men, women, children, infants, oxen, sheep, camels, and donkeys. Saul defeated them but spared Agag the king and kept the best of the livestock. When Samuel confronted him, Saul claimed the people had kept the best animals to sacrifice to the LORD. Samuel's response became one of Scripture's clearest statements on obedience: "Has the LORD as great delight in burnt offerings and sacrifices, as in obeying the voice of the LORD? Behold, to obey is better than sacrifice, and to listen than the fat of rams. For rebellion is as the sin of divination, and presumption is as iniquity and idolatry. Because you have rejected the word of the LORD, he has also rejected you from being king" (1 Samuel 15:22-23).

First Samuel 15:11 records God's assessment: "I regret that I have made Saul king, for he has turned back from following me and has not performed my commandments." The language of God's "regret" anthropomorphizes His response to Saul's failure. It does not suggest God made a mistake or failed to foresee Saul's rebellion. It expresses God's grief over Saul's sin and the necessity of judgment. Samuel grieved for Saul and never saw him again until the day of his death. "And the LORD regretted that he had made Saul king over Israel" (15:35).

What are we to make of Saul? God chose him to be king. God's Spirit came upon him. God gave him victory over Israel's enemies. Yet God rejected him from kingship. He lived decades more as king in name, but God's presence departed. An evil spirit from God tormented him. He descended into paranoid rage, attempting to kill David repeatedly. He consulted a medium at Endor in violation of God's law. He died by suicide on Mount Gilboa, and his body was desecrated by the Philistines.

Saul demonstrates that God can choose someone for temporary service without choosing them for eternal salvation. God appointed Saul to be Israel's first king knowing Saul would fail. The appointment served God's purposes—it showed Israel that having a king "like the nations" would not solve their problems. It prepared the way for David. It demonstrated that the king after God's own heart would be one God chose, not one the people demanded based on impressive appearance. Saul's failure was real, his judgment was just, yet even his failure served God's sovereign purposes for Israel and for the Messianic line that would come through David.

## David Chosen (1 Samuel 16-31)

God sent Samuel to Bethlehem to anoint one of Jesse's sons as king in Saul's place. First Samuel 16:1 makes God's initiative explicit: "I have provided for myself a king among his sons." God had already chosen. Samuel's task was simply to identify and anoint the one God had selected. When Samuel saw Eliab, Jesse's eldest son, he assumed this impressive young man must be God's choice. "Surely the LORD's anointed is before him" (16:6).

But God corrected him immediately: "Do not look on his appearance or on the height of his stature, because I have rejected him. For the LORD sees not as man sees: man looks on the outward appearance, but the LORD looks on the heart" (1 Samuel 16:7). This statement became definitive for understanding how God chooses. Human beings assess based on external qualifications. God assesses based on the heart. But even this requires clarification—God does not choose based on finding a good heart. Rather, God knows the heart He Himself has formed. His choice precedes any quality in the person chosen.

Jesse brought seven sons before Samuel. God rejected all seven. Samuel asked, "Are all your sons here?" Jesse replied that the youngest was keeping the sheep. Samuel commanded, "Send and get him, for we will not sit down till he comes here" (1 Samuel 16:11). When David arrived, God said, "Arise, anoint him, for this is he" (16:12). Samuel anointed him in the midst of his brothers, "and the Spirit of the LORD rushed upon David from that day forward."

The contrast between Saul and David is stark. Saul was tall and impressive; David was the youngest and overlooked. Saul was chosen in response to the people's demand for a king like the nations; David was chosen according to God's own heart. Saul was anointed and the Spirit came upon him temporarily; David was anointed and the Spirit remained on him permanently. Saul was rejected for disobedience; David was sustained through catastrophic sin. The difference was not David's superior character—he would commit adultery and murder. The difference was God's sovereign choice to love David with an unconditional covenant love.

David's relationship with God demonstrated warmth and intimacy from the beginning. When David came to the battlefield and heard Goliath's taunts, he responded with indignation: "Who is this uncircumcised Philistine, that he should defy the armies of the living God?" (1 Samuel 17:26). David's confidence before Goliath came from his experience as a shepherd: "The LORD who delivered me from the paw of the lion and from the paw of the bear will deliver me from the hand of this Philistine" (17:37). He ran toward the giant, struck him with a stone, and cut off his head with Goliath's own sword.

Saul's jealousy began immediately after the victory. The women sang, "Saul has struck down his thousands, and David his ten thousands" (1 Samuel 18:7). From that day, Saul eyed David with suspicion. He tried to pin David to the wall with his spear twice. He sent David on dangerous military missions hoping he would be killed. He gave his daughter Michal to David as wife, hoping she would be a snare to him. He hunted David in the wilderness for years, bringing three thousand chosen men to capture him.

Through all of this, God protected David. When Saul entered the very cave where David was hiding, David's men urged him to kill Saul. David refused: "The LORD forbid that I should do this thing to my lord, the LORD's anointed, to put out my hand against him, seeing he is the LORD's anointed" (1 Samuel 24:6). David cut off a corner of Saul's robe instead, then called out to Saul after he left the cave, showing him the piece of robe and declaring he could have killed him but refused. Saul wept and acknowledged David would be king.

Yet Saul's repentance proved temporary. He resumed hunting David. When David again had opportunity to kill Saul—this time taking his spear and water jar while he slept—David again refused to harm him. "Who can put out his hand against the LORD's anointed and be guiltless?" (1 Samuel 26:9). David's respect for God's anointing extended even to Saul, who was attempting to murder him. David would not take the kingdom by his own hand. He trusted God to give it to him in God's timing.

The text repeatedly emphasizes God's presence with David. "David had success in all his undertakings, for the LORD was with him" (1 Samuel 18:14). When Saul saw this, "he stood in fearful awe of him. But all Israel and Judah loved David, for he went out and came in before them" (18:15-16). The contrast was clear—God was with David and had departed from Saul. This difference explained everything that followed. David's success did not come from his own abilities. It came from God's presence and favor.

The relationship was personal and warm. David's friendship with Jonathan, Saul's son, demonstrated covenant loyalty. They made a covenant together because Jonathan loved David as his own soul (1 Samuel 18:3). When Jonathan learned his father was determined to kill David, he helped David escape and warned him repeatedly of danger. Their final parting in 1 Samuel 20:41-42 showed the depth of their bond: "They kissed one another and wept with one another, David weeping the most. Then Jonathan said to David, 'Go in peace, because we have sworn both of us in the name of the LORD, saying, "The LORD shall be between me and you, and between my offspring and your offspring, forever."'"

This friendship between David and Jonathan pictures the covenant love God had for David. Just as Jonathan loved David at cost to his own prospects for kingship, so God loved David at no benefit to Himself. Jonathan's love was particular—not for all Israel, but for David specifically. It was initiating—Jonathan made the covenant before David had done anything to earn it. It was sustaining—Jonathan protected David repeatedly at great personal risk. It was personal—they wept together, kissed, swore oaths. This is the kind of love—hesed—that God had for David, demonstrated through human friendship but ultimately flowing from divine choice.

---

# 2 SAMUEL: Davidic Covenant Established

Second Samuel opens with David's lament over Saul and Jonathan's deaths. His grief was genuine despite Saul's persecution. David's character throughout 2 Samuel reveals a man after God's own heart—not because he was morally perfect, but because his heart was oriented toward God even through catastrophic sin. The Davidic covenant established in chapter 7 becomes foundational for understanding the Messiah. David's adultery and murder in chapters 11-12 demonstrate hesed operating through the worst atrocities. The discipline that followed—Amnon's rape of Tamar, Absalom's rebellion, Adonijah's attempted coup—showed that God's covenant love includes severe consequences for sin even while the covenant itself holds firm.

## David's Reign (2 Samuel 1-10)

After mourning Saul and Jonathan, David inquired of the LORD about where to go. God directed him to Hebron. The men of Judah came and anointed David king over Judah. Meanwhile, Abner made Saul's son Ish-bosheth king over the northern tribes. Civil war followed for years. David grew stronger while Saul's house grew weaker. Eventually Abner turned to David, and Joab murdered him in revenge for killing his brother. When Ish-bosheth was assassinated, all the tribes came to David at Hebron and anointed him king over all Israel.

David's first acts as king over united Israel demonstrated his priorities. He captured Jerusalem and made it his capital. He brought the ark of God to Jerusalem with great celebration. When Uzzah touched the ark and was struck dead, David feared the LORD that day and delayed bringing the ark further. Three months later, after God blessed the house of Obed-edom where the ark remained, David brought it the rest of the way with sacrifices and dancing. Michal despised him for dancing before the LORD, and David responded that he would be even more undignified than this, for he was celebrating before the LORD who chose him (2 Samuel 6:21-22).

The account of bringing the ark demonstrates David's heart for God. He danced with all his might before the LORD. He leaped and danced, wearing only a linen ephod like a priest rather than royal robes. His worship was uninhibited, wholehearted, and focused entirely on the LORD rather than his own dignity. This is what it meant for David to be a man after God's own heart—not that he was sinless, but that his passion was directed toward God in worship.

Second Samuel 7 records the Davidic covenant—the theological centerpiece of the book. David proposed to Nathan that he wanted to build a house for God. Nathan initially approved, but that night God gave Nathan a different message. God recounted how He had brought David from the pasture to be prince over Israel, how He had been with David wherever he went, how He had cut off David's enemies. Then came the promise: "Moreover, the LORD declares to you that the LORD will make you a house. When your days are fulfilled and you lie down with your fathers, I will raise up your offspring after you, who shall come from your body, and I will establish his kingdom. He shall build a house for my name, and I will establish the throne of his kingdom forever" (2 Samuel 7:11-13).

The covenant continued with even more explicit promises: "I will be to him a father, and he shall be to me a son.
